Long Run Effects of Temporary Incentives on Medical Care Productivity
Pablo Celhay, Paul Gertler, Paula Giovagnoli, and Christel Vermeersch
NBER Working Paper No. 21361
July 2015
JEL No. I11,I13,I15

                                              ABSTRACT

The adoption of new clinical practice patterns by medical care providers is often challenging, even
when they are believed to be both efficacious and profitable. This paper uses a randomized field experiment
to examine the effects of temporary financial incentives paid to medical care clinics for the initiation
of prenatal care in the first trimester of pregnancy. The rate of early initiation of prenatal care was
34% higher in the treatment group than in the control group while the incentives were being paid,
and this effect persisted at least 24 months or more after the incentives ended. These results are consistent
with a model where the incentives enable providers to address the fixed costs of overcoming organizational
inertia in innovation, and suggest that temporary incentives may be effective at motivating improvements
in long run provider performance at a substantially lower cost than permanent incentives.

               4.2                   MEASUREMENT	 Â OF	 Â WEEKS	 Â PREGNANT	 Â AT	 Â 1ST	 Â PRENATAL	 Â VISIT	 Â 

               We	 Â construct	 Â the	 Â number	 Â of	 Â weeks	 Â of	 Â pregnancy	 Â at	 Â the	 Â time	 Â of	 Â the	 Â first	 Â prenatal	 Â visit	 Â as	 Â the	 Â

5 IDENTIFICATION	 Â AND	 Â ESTIMATION	 Â 
We	 Â  estimate	 Â  both	 Â  the	 Â  intent-Â­â€to-Â­â€treat	 Â  (ITT)	 Â  and	 Â  local	 Â  average	 Â  treatment	 Â  (LATE)	 Â  effects	 Â  of	 Â  the	 Â 
incentives	 Â  on	 Â  outcomes.	 Â  The	 Â  ITT	 Â  is	 Â  the	 Â  effect	 Â  of	 Â  assigning	 Â  a	 Â  clinic	 Â  to	 Â  treatment	 Â  on	 Â  outcomes,	 Â 
regardless	 Â of	 Â compliance.	 Â It	 Â compares	 Â the	 Â mean	 Â outcome	 Â of	 Â the	 Â group	 Â assigned	 Â to	 Â treatment	 Â to	 Â the	 Â 
mean	 Â  outcome	 Â  of	 Â  the	 Â  group	 Â  assigned	 Â  to	 Â  control	 Â  and	 Â  is	 Â  estimated	 Â  by	 Â  regressing	 Â  the	 Â  outcome	 Â  against	 Â 
an	 Â  indicator	 Â  of	 Â  whether	 Â  the	 Â  clinic	 Â  was	 Â  assigned	 Â  to	 Â  treatment.	 Â  The	 Â  LATE	 Â  is	 Â  the	 Â  effect	 Â  of	 Â  a	 Â  clinic	 Â 
actually	 Â receiving	 Â the	 Â incentives	 Â and	 Â is	 Â estimated	 Â regressing	 Â the	 Â outcome	 Â against	 Â whether	 Â the	 Â clinic	 Â 
was	 Â  actually	 Â  treated,	 Â  using	 Â  the	 Â  clinicâ€™s	 Â  randomized	 Â  assignment	 Â  status	 Â  as	 Â  an	 Â  instrumental	 Â  variable	 Â 
for	 Â actual	 Â treatment	 Â (Imbens	 Â and	 Â Angrist	 Â 1994).	 Â In	 Â both	 Â cases,	 Â the	 Â treatment	 Â effect	 Â is	 Â identified	 Â off	 Â 
the	 Â variation	 Â induced	 Â by	 Â the	 Â randomized	 Â assignment	 Â status.	 Â In	 Â the	 Â discussion	 Â of	 Â results	 Â in	 Â the	 Â next	 Â 
section,	 Â we	 Â report	 Â the	 Â LATE	 Â estimates.18	 Â 	 Â 




	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â  	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â  	 Â 
                        18	 Â The	 Â ITT	 Â results	 Â are	 Â almost	 Â identical	 Â to	 Â the	 Â LATE	 Â estimates,	 Â which	 Â is	 Â expected	 Â given	 Â the	 Â relatively	 Â high	 Â 

compliance	 Â rates	 Â to	 Â the	 Â original	 Â assignment.	 Â The	 Â ITT	 Â results	 Â are	 Â presented	 Â in	 Â Appendix	 Â C.	 Â 


                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         11	 Â    	 Â

             Our	 Â sample	 Â is	 Â clustered	 Â within	 Â 36	 Â health	 Â clinics	 Â since	 Â the	 Â random	 Â assignment	 Â of	 Â treatment	 Â 
occurred	 Â at	 Â the	 Â clinic	 Â level.	 Â  As	 Â  such,	 Â  there	 Â  may	 Â  be	 Â intra-Â­â€cluster	 Â correlation	 Â that	 Â must	 Â be	 Â considered	 Â 
for	 Â  statistical	 Â  inference.	 Â  Standard	 Â  methods	 Â  of	 Â  correcting	 Â  standard	 Â  errors	 Â  rely	 Â  on	 Â  large	 Â  sample	 Â 
theory	 Â both	 Â in	 Â the	 Â number	 Â of	 Â observations	 Â and	 Â in	 Â the	 Â number	 Â of	 Â clusters.	 Â Given	 Â the	 Â small	 Â number	 Â of	 Â 
clusters	 Â in	 Â our	 Â sample,	 Â we	 Â instead	 Â use	 Â statistical	 Â inference	 Â methods	 Â that	 Â are	 Â robust	 Â to	 Â randomized	 Â 
assignment	 Â of	 Â treatment	 Â among	 Â a	 Â small	 Â number	 Â of	 Â clusters.	 Â Specifically,	 Â we	 Â use	 Â the	 Â Wild	 Â bootstrap	 Â 
method	 Â  to	 Â  generate	 Â  p-Â­â€values	 Â  for	 Â  hypothesis	 Â  testing	 Â  in	 Â  ITT	 Â  models	 Â  (Cameron	 Â  et	 Â  al.	 Â  2008)	 Â  and	 Â  an	 Â 
analogous	 Â  method	 Â  for	 Â  hypothesis	 Â  testing	 Â  in	 Â  the	 Â  LATE	 Â  models	 Â  (Gelbach	 Â  et	 Â  al.	 Â  2009).	 Â  	 Â  Our	 Â  Wild	 Â 
bootstrap	 Â procedure	 Â assigns	 Â symmetric	 Â weights	 Â and	 Â equal	 Â probability	 Â after	 Â re-Â­â€sampling	 Â residuals,	 Â 
and	 Â uses	 Â 999	 Â replications	 Â (Davidson	 Â and	 Â Flachaire	 Â 2008).	 Â

shows	 Â  that	 Â  the	 Â  treatment	 Â  group	 Â  density	 Â  is	 Â  to	 Â  the	 Â  left	 Â  of	 Â  the	 Â  control	 Â  group	 Â  density	 Â  during	 Â  the	 Â

left	 Â  of	 Â  the	 Â  control	 Â  group	 Â  density	 Â  during	 Â  post-Â­â€intervention	 Â  periods	 Â  I	 Â  and	 Â  II.	 Â  	 Â  Kolmogorov-Â­â€Smirnov	 Â 
tests	 Â  for	 Â  equality	 Â  of	 Â  the	 Â  distributions	 Â  cannot	 Â  be	 Â  rejected	 Â  for	 Â  the	 Â  pre-Â­â€intervention	 Â  analysis,	 Â  but	 Â  are	 Â

                                                6.5                                                                      ROBUSTNESS	 Â 

                                                            We	 Â  implement	 Â  three	 Â  robustness	 Â  checks.	 Â  First,	 Â  the	 Â  main	 Â  sample	 Â  may	 Â  include	 Â  pregnancies	 Â

	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â  	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â 	 Â  	 Â

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         14	 Â    	 Â

                                                            Second,	 Â  even	 Â  though	 Â  there	 Â  were	 Â  no	 Â  statistical	 Â  differences	 Â  in	 Â  baseline	 Â  means,	 Â  it	 Â  is	 Â  possible	 Â

probability	 Â of	 Â that	 Â the	 Â first	 Â visit	 Â occurred	 Â before	 Â week	 Â 13	 Â (B2),	 Â respectively.	 Â The	 Â results	 Â are	 Â sorted	 Â

online	 Â survey	 Â administered	 Â to	 Â the	 Â chief	 Â medical	 Â officer	 Â of	 Â each	 Â clinic	 Â about	 Â the	 Â absolute	 Â and	 Â relative	 Â 
importance	 Â  of	 Â  seven	 Â  different	 Â  prenatal	 Â  care	 Â  procedures	 Â  including	 Â  initiating	 Â  prenatal	 Â  care	 Â  prior	 Â  to	 Â 
week	 Â 13	 Â of	 Â pregnancy	 Â (see	 Â Appendix	 Â D).	 Â 

             Figures	 Â  8	 Â  and	 Â  9	 Â  compare	 Â  the	 Â  absolute	 Â  score	 Â and	 Â  relative	 Â  ranking	 Â  of	 Â  the	 Â  procedures	 Â  in	 Â  terms	 Â 
of	 Â  importance	 Â  for	 Â  prenatal	 Â  care.	 Â  The	 Â  absolute	 Â  scores	 Â  ranges	 Â  from	 Â  0	 Â  to	 Â  5,	 Â  with	 Â  5	 Â  being	 Â  the	 Â  highest	 Â 
while	 Â the	 Â relative	 Â ranking	 Â sorts	 Â the	 Â seven	 Â practices	 Â from	 Â 1	 Â to	 Â 7,	 Â with	 Â 1	 Â being	 Â the	 Â highest	 Â ranking.	 Â 
Our	 Â outcomes	 Â of	 Â interest	 Â are	 Â the	 Â absolute	 Â score	 Â and	 Â relative	 Â ranking	 Â assigned	 Â to	 Â early	 Â initiation	 Â of	 Â 
prenatal	 Â care.	 Â Figure	 Â 8	 Â shows	 Â that	 Â the	 Â absolute	 Â score	 Â assigned	 Â to	 Â early	 Â prenatal	 Â care	 Â is	 Â on	 Â average	 Â 
4.8	 Â in	 Â the	 Â treatment	 Â group	 Â and	 Â 4.7	 Â in	 Â the	 Â control	 Â group.	 Â Figure	 Â 9	 Â shows	 Â that	 Â on	 Â average	 Â the	 Â relative	 Â 
ranking	 Â for	 Â this	 Â practice	 Â is	 Â also	 Â similar	 Â between	 Â the	 Â two	 Â groups,	 Â 2.0	 Â for	 Â the	 Â treatment	 Â group	 Â and	 Â 1.9	 Â 
for	 Â  the	 Â  control	 Â  group.	 Â  Moreover,	 Â  these	 Â  differences	 Â  are	 Â  not	 Â  statistically	 Â  significant	 Â  at	 Â  conventional	 Â 
levels	 Â (see	 Â Appendix	 Â D).	 Â These	 Â results	 Â suggest	 Â that	 Â the	 Â early	 Â initiation	 Â of	 Â prenatal	 Â care	 Â is	 Â of	 Â similar	 Â 
high	 Â  absolute	 Â  and	 Â  relative	 Â  importance	 Â  and	 Â  that	 Â  temporary	 Â  fees	 Â  did	 Â  not	 Â  have	 Â  a	 Â  lasting	 Â  effect	 Â  on	 Â 
either	 Â the	 Â absolute	 Â nor	 Â relative	 Â importance.	 Â 	 Â 

          6.8             ALTERNATIVE	 Â EXPLANATIONS	 Â 

          One	 Â  alternative	 Â  explanation	 Â  for	 Â  the	 Â  short-Â­â€term	 Â  treatment	 Â  effects	 Â  is	 Â  that	 Â  the	 Â  incentives	 Â  are	 Â 
causing	 Â treatment	 Â clinics	 Â to	 Â try	 Â to	 Â attract	 Â pregnant	 Â women	 Â who	 Â otherwise	 Â would	 Â have	 Â used	 Â other	 Â 
clinics.	 Â  This	 Â  is	 Â  unlikely	 Â  to	 Â  be	 Â  true	 Â  as	 Â  beneficiary	 Â  women	 Â  are	 Â  assigned	 Â  to	 Â  specific	 Â  clinics	 Â  when	 Â 
enrolled	 Â in	 Â Plan	 Â Nacer.	 Â Moreover,	 Â the	 Â number	 Â of	 Â patients	 Â per	 Â month	 Â and	 Â the	 Â share	 Â that	 Â initiate	 Â care	 Â 
before	 Â week	 Â 13	 Â are	 Â the	 Â same	 Â in	 Â the	 Â pre-Â­â€	 Â and	 Â post-Â­â€intervention	 Â periods	 Â for	 Â control	 Â clinics,	 Â and	 Â the	 Â 
average	 Â monthly	 Â number	 Â of	 Â patients	 Â is	 Â also	 Â the	 Â same	 Â in	 Â the	 Â pre-Â­â€	 Â and	 Â post-Â­â€intervention	 Â periods	 Â for	 Â 
the	 Â treatment	 Â clinics.	 Â 	 Â 


                                                                                   18	 Â                                                                                    	 Â 
	 Â 
	 Â 


          An	 Â alternative	 Â explanation	 Â for	 Â long-Â­â€run	 Â results	 Â is	 Â that	 Â after	 Â the	 Â temporary	 Â incentives	 Â ended,	 Â 
women	 Â who	 Â were	 Â pregnant	 Â during	 Â the	 Â intervention	 Â periods	 Â passed	 Â the	 Â message	 Â of	 Â the	 Â importance	 Â 
of	 Â  early	 Â  initiation	 Â  of	 Â  care	 Â  onto	 Â  other	 Â  beneficiary	 Â  women	 Â  who	 Â  became	 Â  pregnant	 Â  during	 Â  the	 Â  post-Â­â€
intervention	 Â  period.	 Â  Hence,	 Â  the	 Â  persistence	 Â  of	 Â  the	 Â  effect	 Â  of	 Â  the	 Â  incentives	 Â  after	 Â  the	 Â  incentives	 Â  might	 Â 
be	 Â  caused	 Â  by	 Â  an	 Â  informational	 Â  spillover.	 Â  However,	 Â  the	 Â  higher	 Â  amount	 Â  of	 Â  the	 Â  community	 Â  outreach	 Â 
activities	 Â  in	 Â  treatment	 Â  clinics,	 Â  the	 Â  mechanism	 Â  used	 Â  to	 Â  generate	 Â  higher	 Â  early	 Â  initiation	 Â  of	 Â  care,	 Â 
continued	 Â into	 Â the	 Â post-Â­â€experimental	 Â period	 Â at	 Â the	 Â same	 Â level	 Â as	 Â in	 Â the	 Â intervention	 Â period.	 Â Hence,	 Â 
if	 Â there	 Â were	 Â information	 Â spillovers	 Â in	 Â the	 Â post-Â­â€intervention	 Â period,	 Â then	 Â one	 Â would	 Â expect	 Â to	 Â see	 Â

          Finally,	 Â one	 Â might	 Â argue	 Â that	 Â the	 Â clinics	 Â continued	 Â the	 Â new	 Â routines	 Â after	 Â the	 Â temporary	 Â fees	 Â 
were	 Â  eliminated	 Â  because	 Â  they	 Â  faced	 Â  a	 Â  large	 Â  fixed	 Â  cost	 Â  of	 Â  reverting	 Â  to	 Â  the	 Â  old	 Â  routines	 Â  and	 Â  not	 Â 
because	 Â  the	 Â  new	 Â  routines	 Â  added	 Â  net	 Â  value.	 Â  However,	 Â  in	 Â  this	 Â  case,	 Â  we	 Â  think	 Â  that	 Â  the	 Â  fixed	 Â  costs	 Â  of	 Â 
reversing	 Â  the	 Â  routines	 Â  were	 Â  small,	 Â  because	 Â  the	 Â  community	 Â  health	 Â  workers	 Â  could	 Â  simply	 Â  have	 Â 
returned	 Â to	 Â their	 Â old	 Â patterns	 Â of	 Â activities.	 Â 	 Â 


7 CROSS-Â­â€PRICE	 Â EFFECTS	 Â 
            While	 Â the	 Â modified	 Â fee	 Â schedule	 Â was	 Â designed	 Â to	 Â affect	 Â the	 Â timing	 Â of	 Â the	 Â first	 Â prenatal	 Â visit,	 Â

The	 Â results	 Â presented	 Â in	 Â Table	 Â 6	 Â report	 Â no	 Â evidence	 Â of	 Â cross-Â­â€price	 Â effects,	 Â positive	 Â or	 Â negative,	 Â in	 Â 
either	 Â  the	 Â  intervention	 Â  period	 Â  or	 Â  in	 Â  post-Â­â€intervention	 Â  period	 Â  I.	 Â  In	 Â  fact,	 Â  the	 Â  levels	 Â  of	 Â  these	 Â  services	 Â 
appear	 Â to	 Â be	 Â constant	 Â over	 Â time.	 Â While	 Â the	 Â concern	 Â about	 Â crowding-Â­â€out	 Â is	 Â typically	 Â for	 Â a	 Â context	 Â of	 Â 
individual	 Â providers	 Â facing	 Â time	 Â and	 Â effort	 Â constraints,	 Â our	 Â results	 Â are	 Â consistent	 Â with	 Â a	 Â firm	 Â setting	 Â 
where	 Â there	 Â are	 Â no	 Â overall	 Â effort	 Â or	 Â time	 Â constraints.	 Â 


8 BIRTH	 Â OUTCOMES	 Â 
          Next	 Â  we	 Â  address	 Â  the	 Â  question	 Â  of	 Â  whether	 Â  the	 Â  effect	 Â  of	 Â  the	 Â  incentives	 Â  for	 Â  early	 Â  initiation	 Â  of	 Â

                                                There	 Â  are	 Â  a	 Â  number	 Â  of	 Â  possible	 Â  reasons	 Â  for	 Â  this.	 Â  First,	 Â  the	 Â  sample	 Â  could	 Â  be	 Â  too	 Â  small	 Â  to	 Â  be	 Â

prenatal	 Â care	 Â records	 Â with	 Â hospital	 Â medical	 Â records,	 Â it	 Â is	 Â possible	 Â that	 Â the	 Â results	 Â in	 Â Table	 Â 4	 Â do	 Â not	 Â

of	 Â  women	 Â  for	 Â  whom	 Â  hospital	 Â  medical	 Â  records	 Â  are	 Â  available.	 Â  Overall,	 Â  we	 Â  obtain	 Â  similar	 Â  results	 Â  to	 Â 
those	 Â  obtained	 Â  with	 Â  the	 Â  full	 Â  sample.27	 Â  Third,	 Â  despite	 Â  the	 Â  medical	 Â  literature	 Â  and	 Â  CPG	 Â 
recommendation,	 Â  it	 Â  is	 Â  possible	 Â  that	 Â  early	 Â  initiation	 Â  of	 Â  care	 Â  matters	 Â  only	 Â  a	 Â  small	 Â  amount	 Â  for	 Â  the	 Â

risk	 Â patients.	 Â High	 Â risk	 Â patients	 Â include,	 Â among	 Â others,	 Â smokers,	 Â substance	 Â abusers,	 Â those	 Â with	 Â poor	 Â

                                                            In	 Â fact,	 Â this	 Â is	 Â consistent	 Â with	 Â the	 Â small	 Â reduction	 Â in	 Â the	 Â average	 Â weeks	 Â pregnant	 Â at	 Â the	 Â time	 Â

9 DISCUSSION	 Â 

We	 Â  examine	 Â  the	 Â  effects	 Â  of	 Â  temporary	 Â  financial	 Â  incentives	 Â  for	 Â  medical	 Â  care	 Â  providers	 Â  to	 Â  increase	 Â 
early	 Â  initiation	 Â  of	 Â  prenatal	 Â  care	 Â  for	 Â  pregnant	 Â  women	 Â  using	 Â  a	 Â  randomized	 Â  controlled	 Â  trial	 Â  in	 Â 
Argentina.	 Â  The	 Â  intervention	 Â  randomly	 Â  allocates	 Â  a	 Â  three-Â­â€fold	 Â  increase	 Â  in	 Â  the	 Â  fee	 Â  paid	 Â  to	 Â  health	 Â 
facilities	 Â for	 Â each	 Â initial	 Â prenatal	 Â visit	 Â that	 Â occurs	 Â before	 Â week	 Â 13	 Â of	 Â pregnancy.	 Â This	 Â premium	 Â was	 Â 
implemented	 Â  for	 Â  a	 Â  period	 Â  of	 Â  8	 Â  months	 Â  and	 Â  then	 Â  ended.	 Â  Using	 Â  data	 Â  on	 Â  health	 Â  services	 Â  and	 Â  birth	 Â 
outcomes	 Â  from	 Â  medical	 Â  records,	 Â  we	 Â  investigate	 Â  both	 Â  the	 Â  short-Â­â€term	 Â  effects	 Â  of	 Â  the	 Â  incentive	 Â  and	 Â 
whether	 Â the	 Â effects	 Â persist	 Â once	 Â the	 Â direct	 Â monetary	 Â compensation	 Â disappears.	 Â 	 Â 

             Our	 Â  results	 Â  suggest	 Â  that	 Â  the	 Â  temporary	 Â  incentives	 Â  motivated	 Â  long	 Â  run	 Â  changes	 Â  in	 Â 
performance.	 Â We	 Â find	 Â that	 Â the	 Â incentives	 Â led	 Â to	 Â pregnant	 Â women	 Â being	 Â 35%	 Â more	 Â likely	 Â to	 Â initiate	 Â 
prenatal	 Â  care	 Â  before	 Â  week	 Â  13	 Â  and	 Â  that	 Â  the	 Â  higher	 Â  levels	 Â  of	 Â  early	 Â  initiation	 Â  of	 Â  care	 Â  persisted	 Â  for	 Â  at	 Â 
least	 Â  15	 Â  months	 Â  and	 Â  likely	 Â  more	 Â  than	 Â  24	 Â  months	 Â  after	 Â  the	 Â  incentives	 Â  ended.	 Â  These	 Â  results	 Â  are	 Â 
consistent	 Â  with	 Â  a	 Â  model	 Â  of	 Â  providers	 Â  who	 Â  face	 Â  a	 Â  fixed	 Â  cost	 Â  to	 Â  changing	 Â  their	 Â  clinical	 Â  practice	 Â 
routines,	 Â  i.e.	 Â  organizational	 Â  inertia.	 Â  Temporary	 Â  incentives	 Â  induced	 Â  providers	 Â  to	 Â  adopt	 Â  changes	 Â  to	 Â 
their	 Â  clinical	 Â  practice	 Â  patterns	 Â  by	 Â  helping	 Â  them	 Â  to	 Â  overcome	 Â  inertia.	 Â  Once	 Â  they	 Â  adopt	 Â  changes	 Â  to	 Â 
practice	 Â  patterns	 Â  that	 Â  they	 Â  believe	 Â  are	 Â  beneficial	 Â  to	 Â  patients,	 Â  the	 Â  changes	 Â  persist	 Â  even	 Â  after	 Â  the	 Â 
monetary	 Â  incentives	 Â  disappear.	 Â  These	 Â  results	 Â  are	 Â  consistent	 Â  with	 Â  the	 Â  findings	 Â  from	 Â  in-Â­â€depth	 Â 
interviews	 Â that	 Â evidenced	 Â that	 Â treatment	 Â clinics	 Â adopted	 Â innovative	 Â practices	 Â and	 Â changed	 Â routines	 Â 
in	 Â order	 Â to	 Â increase	 Â early	 Â initiation	 Â of	 Â prenatal	 Â care.	 Â 

             Our	 Â study	 Â adds	 Â to	 Â the	 Â growing	 Â body	 Â of	 Â evidence	 Â that	 Â incentives	 Â are	 Â effective	 Â in	 Â improving	 Â 
provider	 Â performance.	 Â 	 Â Our	 Â results	 Â also	 Â have	 Â a	 Â number	 Â of	 Â important	 Â policy	 Â implications.	 Â First,	 Â our	 Â 
results	 Â  suggest	 Â  that	 Â  temporary	 Â  incentives	 Â  may	 Â  be	 Â  effective	 Â  in	 Â  motivating	 Â  long-Â­â€term	 Â  provider	 Â 
performance	 Â  at	 Â  a	 Â  substantially	 Â  lower	 Â  cost	 Â  than	 Â  permanent	 Â  incentives.	 Â  Second,	 Â  while	 Â  we	 Â  find	 Â  that	 Â 
incentives	 Â are	 Â able	 Â to	 Â motivate	 Â changes	 Â in	 Â clinical	 Â practice	 Â patterns,	 Â we	 Â did	 Â not	 Â find	 Â improvements	 Â 
in	 Â health	 Â outcomes.	 Â The	 Â monetary	 Â incentives	 Â that	 Â were	 Â implemented	 Â were	 Â not	 Â able	 Â to	 Â sufficiently	 Â 
reach	 Â those	 Â women	 Â for	 Â whom	 Â early	 Â initiation	 Â of	 Â prenatal	 Â care	 Â would	 Â have	 Â the	 Â largest	 Â health	 Â impact.	 Â 
Therefore,	 Â  incentives	 Â  may	 Â  be	 Â  made	 Â  more	 Â  effective	 Â  by	 Â  defining	 Â  ex-Â­â€ante	 Â  the	 Â  population	 Â  most	 Â  likely	 Â  to	 Â 
benefit,	 Â and	 Â tailoring	 Â incentives	 Â towards	 Â this	 Â population.	 Â However,	 Â tailoring	 Â incentives	 Â to	 Â high	 Â risk	 Â 
populations	 Â  or	 Â  those	 Â  most	 Â  likely	 Â  to	 Â  benefit	 Â  from	 Â  the	 Â  services	 Â  may	 Â  not	 Â  be	 Â  contractible	 Â  as	 Â  these	 Â 
characteristics	 Â  are	 Â  typically	 Â  not	 Â  observable.	 Â  This	 Â  is	 Â  maybe	 Â  a	 Â  major	 Â  limitation	 Â  of	 Â  using	 Â  incentive	 Â 
contracts	 Â to	 Â improve	 Â health	 Â outcomes.	 Â 	 Â 	 Â

APPENDIX	 Â A:	 Â TEST	 Â OF	 Â MISREPORTING	 Â WEEKS	 Â PREGNANT	 Â AT	 Â 1ST	 Â PRENATAL	 Â VISIT	 Â 

One	 Â concern	 Â is	 Â that	 Â the	 Â financial	 Â incentives	 Â may	 Â cause	 Â clinics	 Â to	 Â misreport	 Â the	 Â week	 Â of	 Â pregnancy	 Â at	 Â 
the	 Â first	 Â visit.	 Â In	 Â this	 Â appendix	 Â we	 Â report	 Â the	 Â results	 Â of	 Â test	 Â for	 Â this	 Â behavior.	 Â Recall	 Â that	 Â in	 Â our	 Â main	 Â 
analysis	 Â  we	 Â  construct	 Â  the	 Â  week	 Â  of	 Â  pregnancy	 Â  at	 Â  the	 Â  first	 Â  visit	 Â  using	 Â  the	 Â  date	 Â  of	 Â  the	 Â  first	 Â  visit	 Â  and	 Â  the	 Â 
last	 Â  menstrual	 Â  date	 Â  (LMD)	 Â  as	 Â  reported	 Â  by	 Â  the	 Â  women.	 Â  If	 Â  the	 Â  latter	 Â  is	 Â  not	 Â  available	 Â  we	 Â  use	 Â  the	 Â

are	 Â for	 Â 2-Â­â€sided	 Â hypothesis	 Â tests	 Â of	 Â the	 Â null	 Â that	 Â the	 Â difference	 Â is	 Â equal	 Â to	 Â zero.	 Â We	 Â present	 Â both	 Â the	 Â p-Â­â€value	 Â 
computed	 Â for	 Â large	 Â samples	 Â and	 Â a	 Â Wild	 Â bootstrapped	 Â p-Â­â€value	 Â that	 Â is	 Â robust	 Â in	 Â samples	 Â with	 Â small	 Â numbers	 Â of	 Â

probability	 Â  after	 Â  re-Â­â€sampling	 Â  residuals	 Â  (Davidson	 Â  and	 Â  Flachaire	 Â  2008)	 Â  and	 Â  uses	 Â  999	 Â  replications.	 Â  Column	 Â  (1)	 Â 
reports	 Â the	 Â results	 Â for	 Â the	 Â sample	 Â observed	 Â in	 Â an	 Â 8-Â­â€month	 Â intervention	 Â period	 Â (May	 Â 2010	 Â â€“	 Â December	 Â 2010).	 Â

intervention	 Â  (January	 Â  2011	 Â  â€“	 Â  December	 Â  2011).	 Â  Standard	 Â  errors	 Â  are	 Â  in	 Â  parentheses.	 Â  *	 Â  p	 Â  <	 Â  0.10,	 Â  **	 Â  p	 Â  <	 Â  0.05,	 Â  ***	 Â

APPENDIX	 Â D:	 Â ONLINE	 Â SURVEY	 Â OF	 Â CLINICS	 Â 
	 Â 
             In	 Â collaboration	 Â with	 Â the	 Â Provincial	 Â Management	 Â Unit	 Â of	 Â the	 Â program	 Â (UGPS),	 Â in	 Â May	 Â 2015	 Â 
we	 Â conducted	 Â a	 Â short	 Â online	 Â survey	 Â (using	 Â Survey	 Â MonkeyÂ®)	 Â in	 Â those	 Â clinics	 Â that	 Â participated	 Â in	 Â the	 Â 
pilot.	 Â The	 Â survey	 Â aims	 Â to	 Â measure	 Â the	 Â absolute	 Â and	 Â relative	 Â importance	 Â of	 Â seven	 Â different	 Â prenatal	 Â 
care	 Â  procedures	 Â  including	 Â  initiating	 Â  prenatal	 Â  care	 Â  prior	 Â  to	 Â  week	 Â  13	 Â  of	 Â  pregnancy.	 Â  The	 Â  absolute	 Â 
scores	 Â range	 Â from	 Â 1	 Â to	 Â 5,	 Â with	 Â 5	 Â being	 Â the	 Â highest	 Â score	 Â in	 Â terms	 Â of	 Â importance,	 Â and	 Â an	 Â additional	 Â 
option	 Â  of	 Â  zero	 Â  indicating	 Â  that	 Â  the	 Â  procedure	 Â  is	 Â  not	 Â  appropriate	 Â  for	 Â  a	 Â  pregnant	 Â  woman.	 Â  Hence,	 Â  the	 Â 
absolute	 Â  score	 Â  ranges	 Â  from	 Â  0	 Â  to	 Â  5	 Â  points.	 Â  The	 Â  relative	 Â  ranking	 Â  aimed	 Â  to	 Â  sort	 Â  the	 Â  seven	 Â  practices	 Â 
from	 Â 1	 Â to	 Â 7,	 Â with	 Â 1	 Â being	 Â the	 Â highest	 Â ranking.	 Â 	 Â In	 Â practice	 Â however,	 Â the	 Â survey	 Â instrument	 Â allowed	 Â 
the	 Â respondent	 Â to	 Â repeat	 Â numbers.	 Â 
             The	 Â  survey	 Â  was	 Â  sent	 Â  out	 Â  to	 Â  by	 Â  email	 Â  to	 Â  clinics	 Â  directors	 Â  (or	 Â  the	 Â  next	 Â  person	 Â  in	 Â  rank).	 Â  We	 Â 
were	 Â  unable	 Â  to	 Â  obtain	 Â  current	 Â  email	 Â  addresses	 Â  for	 Â  8	 Â  out	 Â  of	 Â  the	 Â  36	 Â  clinics.	 Â  Another	 Â  4	 Â  clinics	 Â 
confirmed	 Â  having	 Â  received	 Â  the	 Â  email	 Â  but	 Â  refused	 Â  to	 Â  answer	 Â  it.	 Â  Out	 Â  of	 Â  the	 Â  24	 Â  clinics	 Â  that	 Â  did	 Â  respond	 Â 
to	 Â  the	 Â  survey,	 Â  21	 Â  fully	 Â  completed	 Â  it	 Â  while	 Â  3	 Â  only	 Â  partially	 Â  completed	 Â  it.	 Â  Out	 Â  of	 Â  the	 Â  21	 Â  clinics	 Â  with	 Â 
complete	 Â responses,	 Â 13	 Â belong	 Â to	 Â the	 Â treatment	 Â group	 Â and	 Â 8	 Â to	 Â the	 Â control	 Â group.	 Â Appendix	 Â Table	 Â 
D1	 Â  shows	 Â  that	 Â  there	 Â  are	 Â  no	 Â  significant	 Â  differences	 Â  in	 Â  baseline	 Â  characteristics	 Â  between	 Â  clinics	 Â  that	 Â 
responded	 Â to	 Â the	 Â survey	 Â and	 Â clinics	 Â that	 Â did	 Â not	 Â respond.	 Â In	 Â addition,	 Â we	 Â account	 Â for	 Â survey	 Â non-Â­â€
response	 Â  using	 Â  Inverse	 Â  Probability	 Â  Weighting	 Â  based	 Â  on	 Â  the	 Â  logistic	 Â  regression	 Â  reported	 Â  in	 Â  Table	 Â  D2	 Â 
(Wooldridge	 Â 2007).	 Â We	 Â report	 Â results	 Â for	 Â both	 Â IPW	 Â and	 Â non-Â­â€IPW	 Â regressions.	 Â 
             Figures	 Â 8	 Â and	 Â 9	 Â do	 Â not	 Â suggest	 Â any	 Â difference	 Â in	 Â the	 Â absolute	 Â score	 Â and	 Â relative	 Â ranking	 Â of	 Â 
the	 Â procedures	 Â between	 Â treatment	 Â and	 Â control	 Â clinics.	 Â To	 Â test	 Â for	 Â the	 Â significance	 Â of	 Â the	 Â differences	 Â 
between	 Â the	 Â two	 Â groups,	 Â we	 Â run	 Â an	 Â OLS	 Â regression	 Â of	 Â the	 Â absolute	 Â score	 Â and	 Â the	 Â relative	 Â ranking	 Â 
against	 Â a	 Â binary	 Â indicator	 Â for	 Â treatment.	 Â To	 Â account	 Â for	 Â the	 Â small	 Â sample	 Â size	 Â we	 Â also	 Â compute	 Â the	 Â 
p-Â­â€value	 Â  for	 Â  the	 Â  differences	 Â  in	 Â  means	 Â  permuting	 Â  our	 Â  data	 Â  and	 Â  using	 Â  a	 Â  random	 Â  sample	 Â  of	 Â  10,000	 Â 
permutations.	 Â 	 Â The	 Â results	 Â are	 Â shown	 Â in	 Â Tables	 Â D3	 Â and	 Â D4.	 Â 	 Â
